# Tableau and R for Analytics Projects

> On its own, Tableau is a powerful tool that helps professionals analyze, display, and generally make sense of the data at their fingertips. With the addition of R—a free, open-source language for data science—you can glean even more insights from your data. In this course, learn how to combine the analytical strengths of R with the visualization power of Tableau to analyze and present data more ef

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131) | 2h 22m | Intermediate

### 1. Introducing Tableau and R

#### Compare the strengths of Tableau and R
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/compare-the-strengths-of-tableau-and-r?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/compare-the-strengths-of-tableau-and-r?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Tableau is one of the best data visualization software packages around, while R is a powerful command line-driven analytical language.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/compare-the-strengths-of-tableau-and-r?u=76281980&t=9)** The two environments have more in common than you would think, but their strengths lie in two different areas.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/compare-the-strengths-of-tableau-and-r?u=76281980&t=14)** In this movie, I will show you what the two environments look like and compare them.

>

>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/compare-the-strengths-of-tableau-and-r?u=76281980&t=41)** I can also get a summary of what's in the sales data frame, as it's called.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/compare-the-strengths-of-tableau-and-r?u=76281980&t=47)** So, I'll do summary, and then in parentheses, I'll type sales and Enter, and I get a summary of the information.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/compare-the-strengths-of-tableau-and-r?u=76281980&t=56)** So, I have the Minimum, 1st Quartile, Median, Mean, 3rd Quartile, and Maximum values for each of my sales.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/compare-the-strengths-of-tableau-and-r?u=76281980&t=64)** Tableau, on the other hand, lets turn raw data into powerful visualizations.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/compare-the-strengths-of-tableau-and-r?u=76281980&t=69)** You can use those to illustrate patterns and trends in your data.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/compare-the-strengths-of-tableau-and-r?u=76281980&t=73)** To demonstrate, I will switch to Tableau, and here, I have data for a number of customers.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/compare-the-strengths-of-tableau-and-r?u=76281980&t=80)** I have the number of orders they have placed.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/compare-the-strengths-of-tableau-and-r?u=76281980&t=83)** That's called Order Count, and I have that for columns or the horizontal axis, and then the total value of sales.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/compare-the-strengths-of-tableau-and-r?u=76281980&t=92)** And if we look, we can see that there is quite a bit of distribution.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/compare-the-strengths-of-tableau-and-r?u=76281980&t=96)** We have a customer who apparently came in, placed one order, and bought over $1,300 worth of goods.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/compare-the-strengths-of-tableau-and-r?u=76281980&t=104)** And then we have a few who have placed about 30 orders, 26, and bought about 1/3 of that.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/compare-the-strengths-of-tableau-and-r?u=76281980&t=110)** And then finally, we have customers who apparently come in very often and buy a small amount of items, but we really like have them coming in, because they spend a lot of money in our store.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/compare-the-strengths-of-tableau-and-r?u=76281980&t=124)** It's evident that R and Tableau are very different programs, and environments.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/compare-the-strengths-of-tableau-and-r?u=76281980&t=130)** However, when you combine the two of them together, you can create some powerful analyses.

#### How Tableau and R can work together
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Tableau and R seem to represent two ends of the data analysis spectrum.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980&t=5)** Tableau is a user-friendly data visualization program, while R is a data processing language you mainly access through the command line.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980&t=14)** These differences are significant, but they make the combination of the two environments much stronger than either one by itself.

>

>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980&t=90)** It is named Prediction.
>

>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980&t=112)** And actually, I will increase the size of the box just a little.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980&t=117)** It returns an integer.
>

>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980&t=121)** And then the commands tell Tableau and R as a response which CRAN package to use, where the training data should be drawn from, how it should be analyzed using which model.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980&t=136)** And then, because this is a supervised learning algorithm, we need to test it against values with known results.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980&t=144)** So actually what we're doing is we are taking two years of data and then trying to predict the next year.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980&t=151)** So we already know what happened in year 3, but we are trying to predict based on what will happen next time.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980&t=158)** So we have our predictions based on our model, and the -4 means that we have left out our final column of data.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980&t=167)** So the prediction we're making will be added to the field that we create.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980&t=172)** And at the bottom, we have all of the values that are incorporated.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980&t=177)** The first is whether they purchased or not.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980&t=179)** That's either a one or a zero.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980&t=181)** Then Income and Age, which we're using as input variables.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980&t=185)** And then finally, we have a -1.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980&t=187)** And we subtract 1 because when we run this R algorithm through Tableau, it actually returns either a one or a two.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980&t=197)** One meaning no, and two meaning yes.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980&t=200)** So to correspond to the data that we already have in our system with a zero or one, we need to subtract 1 to get the expected result.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980&t=209)** There are a lot of little differences between R, the way that you run it in the console and the way that you run it in Tableau, and this is just one of them.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980&t=217)** I'll try to point out as many as I can along the way.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/how-tableau-and-r-can-work-together?u=76281980&t=220)** We're done here, so I can close out and continue with my work.

>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/import-data-into-r?u=76281980&t=117)** The first is that rather than double quotes, I need to have single quotes.

>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/import-data-into-r?u=76281980&t=126)** So I have single quotes.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/import-data-into-r?u=76281980&t=127)** And the second is that I need to have each of these back slashes, which occur between chapter two and sales trend.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/import-data-into-r?u=76281980&t=137)** And here between exercise files and chapter two, I need to have two of them instead of only one.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/import-data-into-r?u=76281980&t=144)** And the reason is that in R, a backslash is known as a command or control character, and if we didn't have a second backslash, then R would try to read backslash S backslash C as commands they needed or that the engine needed to perform.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/import-data-into-r?u=76281980&t=163)** That's not the case.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/import-data-into-r?u=76281980&t=164)** So I will add a backslash before or after each of the existing ones so that they are doubled up.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/import-data-into-r?u=76281980&t=176)** And R will know that we are not looking for commands.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/import-data-into-r?u=76281980&t=180)** Instead, we are treating the backslash as an actual backslash.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/import-data-into-r?u=76281980&t=185)** Now I will have a comma.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/import-data-into-r?u=76281980&t=188)** The separator for a comma separated value file is in fact a comma.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/import-data-into-r?u=76281980&t=193)** And I'll actually make the window a little bit wider so I have room to type.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/import-data-into-r?u=76281980&t=197)** I can indicate the separator character, using the SEP argument equal, then the single quotes, a comma, then a comma again to move to the next argument.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/import-data-into-r?u=76281980&t=208)** And I know that my data has a header, so I'll type header equals capital T.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/import-data-into-r?u=76281980&t=214)** So that means that there is a header because I've set the header argument to true.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/import-data-into-r?u=76281980&t=222)** Then a right parentheses, and if I did everything right, then I should have R import my table to revenue.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/import-data-into-r?u=76281980&t=232)** I didn't get an error.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/import-data-into-r?u=76281980&t=233)** So let's see what the revenue variable looks like.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/import-data-into-r?u=76281980&t=238)** You can do that using the DIM function.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/import-data-into-r?u=76281980&t=241)** So DIM, and then in parentheses, revenue, press enter, and they get a summary of 36 rows and two columns.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/import-data-into-r?u=76281980&t=252)** That sounds like the data that I wanted to import.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/import-data-into-r?u=76281980&t=255)** So I've brought it into R successfully and can perform calculations on it.

> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/import-data-into-tableau?u=76281980&t=168)** If you need to connect to a server, it's possible you might need to enter your credentials such as a username or a password.

### 3. Creating and Visualizing Linear Regression Models

#### Describe linear regression and multiple regression models
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-linear-regression-and-multiple-regression-models?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-linear-regression-and-multiple-regression-models?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Linear regression models identify trends in your data, whether it's sales over time, the length of time a customer has made purchases versus yearly sales, and so on.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-linear-regression-and-multiple-regression-models?u=76281980&t=11)** In this movie, I will describe the process behind linear regression models and how to evaluate them.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-linear-regression-and-multiple-regression-models?u=76281980&t=17)** The goal is to create the line of best fit to describe data.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-linear-regression-and-multiple-regression-models?u=76281980&t=23)** So you go through a procedure to generate an equation that is used to draw a line, and the goal is to minimize the total errors over all points.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-linear-regression-and-multiple-regression-models?u=76281980&t=34)** So you want to find the line of best fit, and then you will evaluate it based on how well it does, and you can use one or multiple variables.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-linear-regression-and-multiple-regression-models?u=76281980&t=44)** Let's see an example of linear regression that I have created using sales data.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-linear-regression-and-multiple-regression-models?u=76281980&t=50)** So on the X axis, I have the number of orders, and then that is the number of times a customer bought from us in a given year.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-linear-regression-and-multiple-regression-models?u=76281980&t=59)** And then on the Y or vertical axis, I have the total of their sales.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-linear-regression-and-multiple-regression-models?u=76281980&t=65)** So that would be the value.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-linear-regression-and-multiple-regression-models?u=76281980&t=67)** And what you can see if you look to the left is that for individuals who made a small number of purchases, there is absolutely no pattern.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-linear-regression-and-multiple-regression-models?u=76281980&t=75)** However, the more that they buy, the higher the correlation between the number of purchases and the total value of their sales.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-linear-regression-and-multiple-regression-models?u=76281980&t=86)** The equation that was used to draw this line is 8.0339x plus 573.59, and that means that the sales total can be approximated, that is the line of best fit, by multiplying X, the number of orders by 8.0339, and adding 573.59.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-linear-regression-and-multiple-regression-models?u=76281980&t=110)** So if I had a customer with one order, then we would predict that their total sale would be about $582.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-linear-regression-and-multiple-regression-models?u=76281980&t=120)** The R squared value tells you approximately how much of sales value is explained by the X variable.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-linear-regression-and-multiple-regression-models?u=76281980&t=130)** In this case, that is the number of orders.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-linear-regression-and-multiple-regression-models?u=76281980&t=133)** So here it appears that we have an R squared of about 0.42 or about 42%.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-linear-regression-and-multiple-regression-models?u=76281980&t=139)** So while this model seems to do well beyond about 20 orders, it does terribly when we're looking at single orders because those values, as you see, are all over the place.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-linear-regression-and-multiple-regression-models?u=76281980&t=152)** So the lesson to take away from this is that linear regression is a powerful tool, but you need to be careful about how you use it.

#### Create single- and multiple-variable linear regression models
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/create-single-and-multiple-variable-linear-regression-models?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/create-single-and-multiple-variable-linear-regression-models?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Linear regression, which finds the line that best fits a data set is one of the most useful forms of data analysis you can perform.
>

> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=245)** And for every additional order that a customer has placed, then that will be an increase of 10.621 for each order.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=254)** So it appears that order count has a much higher impact than distance traveled based on total sales.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=263)** If I want to see how well each of these variables that is distance and order count are at predicting, I can find a summary of the model.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=272)** So I'll create that summary by typing summary model2.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=278)** That's in parentheses and enter.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=281)** And there we are.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=283)** So let's take a look at what we have.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=286)** The residuals, which are here, are the errors.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=290)** So it appears that we have quite a large range in our errors.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=295)** We are too low by $543.30.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=300)** The average error isn't too bad, it's about $32.8, but the maximum is 369.16.
>
> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=308)** So what that tells me is that this model as a whole is not terribly predictive.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=314)** Now we can go down and look at our coefficients.
>
> **[5:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=317)** So the intercept we don't need to worry about.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=321)** And now let's take a look at how well distance and order count predict.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=326)** So the R value, which tends to be considered 0.05 or lower as significant for distance is 0.01.
>
> **[5:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=336)** So that's actually better than I thought it would be.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=338)** So it is reasonably predictive.
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=340)** Perhaps customers who drive farther to come to the store are more likely to spend more money and those who live closer make a number of small purchases.
>
> **[5:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=349)** Order count, however, is extremely significant and has a very low R value.
>
> **[5:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=355)** So what that tells me is that the two values combined do a pretty good job, but if I had to pick only one, it would be order count.
>
> **[6:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=363)** Now let's take a look down below at the multiple R squared and adjusted R squared value.
>
> **[6:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=371)** R squared tells you as a percentage about how much of the predicted variable, in this case sales, is actually predicted by the model.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=380)** And of course, it's a mathematical estimate.
>
> **[6:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=382)** So here we have a multiple R squared of about 61 or 0.61 or 61%.
>
> **[6:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=391)** However, because we have multiple input variables, we need to use the adjusted R squared.
>
> **[6:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=396)** And the reason for that is when you run the basic R squared calculation with multiple inputs, R squared always goes up.
>
> **[6:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=405)** It's a part of the process.
>
> **[6:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=408)** So instead we corrected that using adjusted R squared.
>
> **[6:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=411)** And so we have a predictive capacity of about 56%.
>
> **[6:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/analyze-regression-variables-for-significance?u=76281980&t=416)** That's not too bad for only two variables, but it is very likely that we could do better by collecting more data about our customers.

### 4. Classifying Data Using Logistic Regression

#### Describe logistic regression algorithms
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-logistic-regression-algorithms?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-logistic-regression-algorithms?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Logistic regression is a supervised training algorithm that builds a model to distinguish between categories of information.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-logistic-regression-algorithms?u=76281980&t=9)** Supervised means that you have a set of known results, for example, customers who donated last year versus those who didn't, and then you build a model to try to predict based on past data.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-logistic-regression-algorithms?u=76281980&t=22)** Again, the goal is to separate entities into groups, and it is possible to perform logistic regression on more than two groups, but we'll stay with the base case.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-logistic-regression-algorithms?u=76281980&t=34)** You train your model, and then test and evaluate it.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-logistic-regression-algorithms?u=76281980&t=38)** Typically, you will divide your data into about 70% training and 30% test sets.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-logistic-regression-algorithms?u=76281980&t=45)** And again, because you know the results in advance, you'll have a very good idea of how well your algorithm is performing.

### 5. Classifying Data Using Support Vector Machines

#### Describe support vector machine algorithms
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-support-vector-machine-algorithms?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-support-vector-machine-algorithms?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] In the previous chapter, we looked at logistic regression as a means of predicting whether a customer, patient, or other entity would be more likely than not to be part of a class of objects.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-support-vector-machine-algorithms?u=76281980&t=12)** We can also use support vector machines.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-support-vector-machine-algorithms?u=76281980&t=15)** There are two basic ways that you can define your models.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-support-vector-machine-algorithms?u=76281980&t=18)** The first is the linear model, where you try to separate items using a straight line.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-support-vector-machine-algorithms?u=76281980&t=24)** Or, if you want to add more parameters, you don't have to have a straight line, and that is non-linear.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-support-vector-machine-algorithms?u=76281980&t=31)** I will concentrate on a straightforward, linear, either/or case.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-support-vector-machine-algorithms?u=76281980&t=35)** It's a great way to be introduced to support vector machines, and I think you'll see immediately how useful they are.

> **Code Keywords:** case, (1), type = (1), switch (1)
> **CLI Commands:** find (1), make (1)
> **Code Identifiers:** svm_test (2)
> **Env Vars:** cran (2)
> **Cross-References:** go back to (1), in the next (1)
> **Exercise Files:** download the (1), exercise files (1)
> **Prerequisites:** install (2)

### 6. Grouping Data Using Cluster Analysis

### 7. Classifying Data Using Random Forests

#### Describe random forest analysis
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-random-forest-analysis?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-random-forest-analysis?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this chapter, we will look at random forest algorithms, which classify entities according to characteristics contained within a dataset.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-random-forest-analysis?u=76281980&t=9)** Specifically, random forest classifies objects by creating decision trees, but it doesn't just create a single decision tree.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-random-forest-analysis?u=76281980&t=17)** What it does is randomly generate trees and test their performance against a test set of data.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-random-forest-analysis?u=76281980&t=23)** So what will happen is that the algorithm will generate a bunch of random solutions, get rid of the ones that don't work well, and recombine the ones that do to try to find ever better solutions.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-random-forest-analysis?u=76281980&t=36)** If you have category data represented as numbers, you need to add in a specific command to ensure that the R package does what it needs to do correctly, but it's just a single bit of code and takes no time at all to add.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-random-forest-analysis?u=76281980&t=52)** The dataset we'll use is from the Glass Identification Dataset that is hosted at the University of California Irvine Machine Learning Repository.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-random-forest-analysis?u=76281980&t=62)** That's available online, and you can go to the URL shown above and through the generosity of UC Irvine if we scroll down, we see that the license allows us to use the data for any purpose whatsoever as long as appropriate credit is given.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/describe-random-forest-analysis?u=76281980&t=79)** So to the University of California Irvine, I thank you and I look forward to working with your data.

>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-and-r-for-analytics-projects-27845131/create-a-random-forest-analysis-model-in-r?u=76281980&t=10)** Testing your values lets you guard against overfitting, which happens when you build a model that is too closely tied to your training dataset and could provide inaccurate results on data you collect later.
